   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    International Phonetic Alphabet
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div class="noprint" id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>

    </div>

       <a href="/wiki/NATO_phonetic_alphabet" title="NATO phonetic alphabet">
        NATO phonetic alphabet
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Americanist_phonetic_notation" title="Americanist phonetic notation">
        Americanist phonetic notation
       </a>
       . For usage of IPA in Wikipedia, see
       <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">
        Help:IPA
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Introduction" title="Help:IPA/Introduction">
        Help:IPA/Introduction
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
        Help:IPA/English
       </a>
       . For other uses, see
       <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/IPA_(disambiguation)" title="IPA (disambiguation)">
        IPA (disambiguation)
       </a>
       .

        <td style="width:1px">
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="vertical-align:middle; font-size:95%;">
         <b>
          This article contains
          <a href="/wiki/Phonetics" title="Phonetics">
           phonetic
          </a>
          <a href="/wiki/Symbol" title="Symbol">
           symbols
          </a>
          .
         </b>
         Without proper
         <a href="/wiki/Help:Special_characters" title="Help:Special characters">
          rendering support
         </a>
         , you may see
         <a href="/wiki/Specials_(Unicode_block)#Replacement_character" title="Specials (Unicode block)">
          question marks, boxes, or other symbols
         </a>
         instead of phonetic symbols.
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       The
       <b>
        International Phonetic Alphabet
       </b>
       (
       <b>
        IPA
       </b>
       ) is an
       <a href="/wiki/Alphabet" title="Alphabet">
        alphabetic
       </a>
       system of
       <a href="/wiki/Phonetic_transcription" title="Phonetic transcription">
        phonetic notation
       </a>
       based primarily on the
       <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">
        Latin alphabet
       </a>
       . It was devised by the
       <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Association" title="International Phonetic Association">
        International Phonetic Association
       </a>
       in the late
       <a href="/wiki/19th_century" title="19th century">
        19th century
       </a>
       as a standardized representation of the sounds of
       <a href="/wiki/Spoken_language" title="Spoken language">
        spoken language
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPA_1999_1-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-IPA_1999-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The IPA is used by
       <a href="/wiki/Lexicography" title="Lexicography">
        lexicographers
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Foreign_language" title="Foreign language">
        foreign language
       </a>
       students and teachers,
       <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">
        linguists
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Speech-language_pathology" title="Speech-language pathology">
        speech-language pathologists
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Singing" title="Singing">
        singers
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Actor" title="Actor">
        actors
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Constructed_language" title="Constructed language">
        constructed language
       </a>
       creators and
       <a href="/wiki/Translation" title="Translation">
        translators
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-world_2-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-world-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The IPA is designed to represent only those qualities of speech that are part of oral language:
       <a href="/wiki/Phone_(phonetics)" title="Phone (phonetics)">
        phones
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">
        phonemes
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Intonation_(linguistics)" title="Intonation (linguistics)">
        intonation
       </a>
       and the separation of
       <a href="/wiki/Word" title="Word">
        words
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Syllable" title="Syllable">
        syllables
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPA_1999_1-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-IPA_1999-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       To represent additional qualities of speech, such as tooth gnashing,
       <a href="/wiki/Lisp" title="Lisp">
        lisping
       </a>
       , and sounds made with a
       <a href="/wiki/Cleft_lip_and_cleft_palate" title="Cleft lip and cleft palate">
        cleft lip and cleft palate
       </a>
       , an extended set of symbols, the
       <a href="/wiki/Extensions_to_the_International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="Extensions to the International Phonetic Alphabet">
        extensions to the International Phonetic Alphabet
       </a>
       , may be used.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-world_2-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-world-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       IPA symbols are composed of one or more elements of two basic types,
       <a href="/wiki/Letter_(alphabet)" title="Letter (alphabet)">
        letters
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Diacritic" title="Diacritic">
        diacritics
       </a>
       . For example, the sound of the English letter ⟨t⟩ may be transcribed in IPA with a single letter,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [t]
       </span>
       , or with a letter plus diacritics,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [t̺ʰ]
       </span>
       , depending on how precise one wishes to be.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [note 1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Often, slashes are used to signal
       <a href="/wiki/Phonetic_transcription#Narrow_versus_broad_transcription" title="Phonetic transcription">
        broad or phonemic transcription
       </a>
       ; thus,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /t/
       </span>
       is less specific than, and could refer to, either
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [t̺ʰ]
       </span>
       or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [t]
       </span>
       , depending on the context and language.
      </p>
      <p>
       Occasionally letters or diacritics are added, removed or modified by the International Phonetic Association. As of the most recent change in 2005,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       there are 107 letters, 52 diacritics and four
       <a href="/wiki/Prosody_(linguistics)" title="Prosody (linguistics)">
        prosodic
       </a>
       marks in the IPA. These are shown in the current IPA chart, posted below in this article and at the website of the IPA.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="History of the International Phonetic Alphabet">
        History of the International Phonetic Alphabet
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In 1886, a group of
       <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">
        French
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">
        British
       </a>
       language teachers, led by the French linguist
       <a href="/wiki/Paul_Passy" title="Paul Passy">
        Paul Passy
       </a>
       , formed what would come to be known from 1897 onwards as the
       <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Association" title="International Phonetic Association">
        International Phonetic Association
       </a>
       (in French,
       <i>
        <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr">
         l’Association phonétique internationale
        </span>
       </i>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPA194-196_7-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-IPA194-196-7">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Their original alphabet was based on a
       <a href="/wiki/Spelling_reform" title="Spelling reform">
        spelling reform
       </a>
       for English known as the
       <a href="/wiki/Romic_alphabet" title="Romic alphabet">
        Romic alphabet
       </a>
       , but in order to make it usable for other languages, the values of the symbols were allowed to vary from language to language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       For example, the sound
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [
        <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_postalveolar_fricative" title="Voiceless postalveolar fricative">
         ʃ
        </a>
        ]
       </span>
       (the
       <i>
        sh
       </i>
       in
       <i>
        shoe
       </i>
       ) was originally represented with the letter ⟨c⟩ in English, but with the digraph ⟨
       <i>
        <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr">
         ch
        </span>
       </i>
       ⟩ in French.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPA194-196_7-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-IPA194-196-7">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, in 1888, the alphabet was revised so as to be uniform across languages, thus providing the base for all future revisions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPA194-196_7-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-IPA194-196-7">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The idea of making the IPA was first suggested by
       <a href="/wiki/Otto_Jespersen" title="Otto Jespersen">
        Otto Jespersen
       </a>
       in a letter to Paul Passy. It was developed by
       <a href="/wiki/Alexander_John_Ellis" title="Alexander John Ellis">
        Alexander John Ellis
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Henry_Sweet" title="Henry Sweet">
        Henry Sweet
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Jones_(phonetician)" title="Daniel Jones (phonetician)">
        Daniel Jones
       </a>
       , and Passy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Since its creation, the IPA has undergone a number of revisions. After major revisions and expansions in
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_International_Phonetic_Alphabet#1900_expansion" title="History of the International Phonetic Alphabet">
        1900
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_International_Phonetic_Alphabet#1932_revision" title="History of the International Phonetic Alphabet">
        1932
       </a>
       , the IPA remained unchanged until the
       <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Association_Kiel_Convention" title="International Phonetic Association Kiel Convention">
        International Phonetic Association Kiel Convention
       </a>
       in 1989. A minor revision took place in 1993 with the addition of four letters for
       <a href="/wiki/Mid_central_vowel" title="Mid central vowel">
        mid central vowels
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-world_2-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-world-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and the removal of letters for
       <a href="/wiki/Implosive_consonant#Voiceless_implosives" title="Implosive consonant">
        voiceless implosives
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pullum_11-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Pullum-11">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The alphabet was last revised in May 2005 with the addition of a letter for a
       <a href="/wiki/Labiodental_flap" title="Labiodental flap">
        labiodental flap
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Apart from the addition and removal of symbols, changes to the IPA have consisted largely of renaming symbols and categories and in modifying typefaces.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-world_2-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-world-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Extensions_to_the_International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="Extensions to the International Phonetic Alphabet">
        Extensions to the International Phonetic Alphabet
       </a>
       for speech pathology were created in 1990 and officially adopted by the
       <a href="/wiki/International_Clinical_Phonetics_and_Linguistics_Association" title="International Clinical Phonetics and Linguistics Association">
        International Clinical Phonetics and Linguistics Association
       </a>
       in 1994.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Description">
        Description
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Description">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_International_Phonetic_Alphabet_(revised_to_2015).pdf">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1650" data-file-width="1275" height="388" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/The_International_Phonetic_Alphabet_%28revised_to_2015%29.pdf/page1-300px-The_International_Phonetic_Alphabet_%28revised_to_2015%29.pdf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/The_International_Phonetic_Alphabet_%28revised_to_2015%29.pdf/page1-450px-The_International_Phonetic_Alphabet_%28revised_to_2015%29.pdf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/The_International_Phonetic_Alphabet_%28revised_to_2015%29.pdf/page1-600px-The_International_Phonetic_Alphabet_%28revised_to_2015%29.pdf.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_International_Phonetic_Alphabet_(revised_to_2015).pdf" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The official chart of the IPA as of 2015.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       For a guide to pronouncing IPA transcriptions of English words, see
       <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet_chart_for_English_dialects" title="International Phonetic Alphabet chart for English dialects">
        International Phonetic Alphabet chart for English dialects
       </a>
       .
      </div>
      <p>
       The general principle of the IPA is to provide one letter for each distinctive sound (
       <a href="/wiki/Segment_(linguistics)" title="Segment (linguistics)">
        speech segment
       </a>
       ), although this practice is not followed if the sound itself is complex.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This means that:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        It does not normally use
        <a href="/wiki/Multigraph_(orthography)" title="Multigraph (orthography)">
         combinations of letters
        </a>
        to represent single sounds, the way
        <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
         English
        </a>
        does with ⟨sh⟩, ⟨th⟩ and ⟨ng⟩, or single letters to represent multiple sounds the way ⟨x⟩ represents
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ks/
        </span>
        or
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɡz/
        </span>
        in English.
       </li>
       <li>
        There are no letters that have context-dependent sound values, as do "hard" and "soft"
        <a href="/wiki/Hard_and_soft_C" title="Hard and soft C">
         ⟨c⟩
        </a>
        or
        <a href="/wiki/Hard_and_soft_G" title="Hard and soft G">
         ⟨g⟩
        </a>
        in several European languages.
       </li>
       <li>
        Finally, the IPA does not usually have separate letters for two sounds if no known language makes a distinction between them, a property known as "selectiveness".
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-world_2-4">
         <a href="#cite_note-world-2">
          [2]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
         <a href="#cite_note-15">
          [note 2]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Among the symbols of the IPA, 107 letters represent
       <a href="/wiki/Consonant" title="Consonant">
        consonants
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">
        vowels
       </a>
       , 31
       <a href="/wiki/Diacritic" title="Diacritic">
        diacritics
       </a>
       are used to modify these, and 19 additional signs indicate
       <a href="/wiki/Segment_(linguistics)" title="Segment (linguistics)">
        suprasegmental
       </a>
       qualities such as
       <a href="/wiki/Length_(phonetics)" title="Length (phonetics)">
        length
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tone_(linguistics)" title="Tone (linguistics)">
        tone
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Stress_(linguistics)" title="Stress (linguistics)">
        stress
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Intonation_(linguistics)" title="Intonation (linguistics)">
        intonation
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [note 3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These are organized into a chart; the chart displayed here is the
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.internationalphoneticassociation.org/content/ipa-chart" rel="nofollow">
        official chart
       </a>
       as posted at the website of the IPA.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Letter_forms">
        Letter forms
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Letter forms">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The letters chosen for the IPA are meant to harmonize with the
       <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">
        Latin alphabet
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [note 4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       For this reason, most letters are either
       <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">
        Latin
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_alphabet" title="Greek alphabet">
        Greek
       </a>
       , or modifications thereof. Some letters are neither: for example, the letter denoting the
       <a href="/wiki/Glottal_stop" title="Glottal stop">
        glottal stop
       </a>
       , ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ʔ
       </span>
       ⟩, has the form of a dotless
       <a href="/wiki/Question_mark" title="Question mark">
        question mark
       </a>
       , and derives originally from an
       <a href="/wiki/Apostrophe" title="Apostrophe">
        apostrophe
       </a>
       . A few letters, such as that of the voiced
       <a href="/wiki/Pharyngeal_consonant" title="Pharyngeal consonant">
        pharyngeal fricative
       </a>
       , ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ʕ
       </span>
       ⟩, were inspired by other writing systems (in this case, the
       <a href="/wiki/Arabic_script" title="Arabic script">
        Arabic
       </a>
       letter
       <span dir="rtl" lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">
        <a href="/wiki/Ayin" title="Ayin">
         ﻉ
        </a>
       </span>
       ‎
       <i>
        ‘ain
       </i>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pullum_11-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Pullum-11">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Despite its preference for harmonizing with the Latin script, the International Phonetic Association has occasionally admitted other letters. For example, before 1989, the IPA letters for
       <a href="/wiki/Click_consonant" title="Click consonant">
        click consonants
       </a>
       were ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Bilabial_clicks" title="Bilabial clicks">
         ʘ
        </a>
       </span>
       ⟩, ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Dental_clicks" title="Dental clicks">
         ʇ
        </a>
       </span>
       ⟩, ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Alveolar_clicks" title="Alveolar clicks">
         ʗ
        </a>
       </span>
       ⟩, and ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Lateral_clicks" title="Lateral clicks">
         ʖ
        </a>
       </span>
       ⟩, all of which were derived either from existing IPA letters, or from Latin and Greek letters. However, except for ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ʘ
       </span>
       ⟩, none of these letters were widely used among
       <a href="/wiki/Khoisan_languages" title="Khoisan languages">
        Khoisanists
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">
        Bantuists
       </a>
       , and as a result they were replaced by the more widespread symbols ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Bilabial_clicks" title="Bilabial clicks">
         ʘ
        </a>
       </span>
       ⟩, ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Dental_clicks" title="Dental clicks">
         ǀ
        </a>
       </span>
       ⟩, ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Alveolar_clicks" title="Alveolar clicks">
         ǃ
        </a>
       </span>
       ⟩, ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Palatal_clicks" title="Palatal clicks">
         ǂ
        </a>
       </span>
       ⟩, and ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Lateral_clicks" title="Lateral clicks">
         ǁ
        </a>
       </span>
       ⟩ at the IPA Kiel Convention in 1989.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Although the IPA diacritics are fully
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Featural_alphabet" title="Featural alphabet">
        featural
       </a>
       , there is little systemicity in the letter forms. A
       <a href="/wiki/Retroflex_consonant" title="Retroflex consonant">
        retroflex
       </a>
       articulation is consistently indicated with a right-swinging tail, as in ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɖ ʂ ɳ
       </span>
       ⟩, and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glottalic_ingressive" title="Glottalic ingressive">
        implosion
       </a>
       by a top hook, ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɓ ɗ ɠ
       </span>
       ⟩, but other pseudo-featural elements are due to haphazard derivation and coincidence. For example, all nasal consonants but uvular ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɴ
       </span>
       ⟩ are based on the form ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        n
       </span>
       ⟩: ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        m ɱ n ɳ ɲ ŋ
       </span>
       ⟩. However, the similarity between ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        m
       </span>
       ⟩ and ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        n
       </span>
       ⟩ is a historical accident; ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɲ
       </span>
       ⟩ and ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ŋ
       </span>
       ⟩ are derived from
       <a href="/wiki/Typographic_ligature" title="Typographic ligature">
        ligatures
       </a>
       of
       <i>
        gn
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        ng,
       </i>
       and ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɱ
       </span>
       ⟩ is an
       <i>
        ad hoc
       </i>
       imitation of ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ŋ
       </span>
       ⟩.
      </p>
      <p>
       Some of the new letters were ordinary Latin letters turned 180 degrees, such as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɐ ɔ ə ɟ ɥ ɯ ɹ ʇ ʌ ʍ ʎ
       </span>
       (turned
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        a c e f h m r t v w y
       </span>
       ). This was easily done in the era of
       <a href="/wiki/Hot_metal_typesetting" title="Hot metal typesetting">
        mechanical typesetting
       </a>
       , and had the advantage of not requiring the casting of special type for IPA symbols.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Capital_letters">
        Capital letters
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Capital letters">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Full capital letters are not used as IPA symbols. They are, however, often used for
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archiphoneme" title="Archiphoneme">
        archiphonemes
       </a>
       and for natural classes of phonemes (that is, as wildcards). Such usage is not part of the IPA or even standardized, and may be ambiguous between authors, but it is commonly used in conjunction with the IPA. (The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ExtIPA" title="ExtIPA">
        extIPA
       </a>
       chart, for example, uses one or two wildcards in its illustrations.) Capital letters are also basic to the
       <a href="/wiki/Voice_Quality_Symbols" title="Voice Quality Symbols">
        Voice Quality Symbols
       </a>
       sometimes used in conjunction with the IPA.
      </p>
      <p>
       As wildcards,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        C
       </span>
       for {consonant} and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        V
       </span>
       for {vowel} are ubiquitous. Other common capital-letter symbols are
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        T
       </span>
       for {tone},
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        N
       </span>
       for {nasal},
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        F
       </span>
       for {fricative} (also
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        S
       </span>
       for {voiceless fricative} and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        Z
       </span>
       for {voiced fricative}),
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        G
       </span>
       for {glide} or for {semivowel/liquid},
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        P
       </span>
       for {plosive} (stop) (also
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        T
       </span>
       for {voiceless stop} and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        D
       </span>
       for {voiced stop}),
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        S
       </span>
       for {sibilant},
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        L
       </span>
       for {liquid} (or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        R
       </span>
       for {rhotic} and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        L
       </span>
       for {lateral}),
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        #
       </span>
       or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        Ʞ
       </span>
       for {click},
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        A
       </span>
       for {open vowel},
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        U
       </span>
       for {rounded vowel} and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        B, D, J or Ɉ, K, Q, Φ, H
       </span>
       for {labial}, {alveolar}, {post-alveolar} or {palatal}, {velar}, {uvular}, {pharyngeal} and {glottal}, respectively, and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        X
       </span>
       for anything. For example, the possible syllable shapes of Mandarin can be abstracted as ranging from
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        V
       </span>
       (atonic vowel) to
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        CVNᵀ
       </span>
       (consonant-vowel-nasal syllable with tone). The letters can be modified with IPA diacritics, for e.g.
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        Cʼ
       </span>
       for {ejective},
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        Ƈ
       </span>
       for {implosive},
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        N͡C
       </span>
       or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ᴺC
       </span>
       for {prenasalized consonant},
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        Ṽ
       </span>
       for {nasal vowel},
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        S̬
       </span>
       for {voiced sibilant},
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        N̥
       </span>
       for {voiceless nasal},
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        P͡F
       </span>
       or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        P
        <sup>
         F
        </sup>
       </span>
       for {affricate} and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        D̪
       </span>
       for {dental consonant}. In speech pathology, they may represent indeterminate sounds, and superscripted when weakly articulated:
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ᴰ
       </span>
       a weak indeterminate alveolar,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ᴷ
       </span>
       a weak indeterminate velar, etc.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Typical examples of archiphonemic use of capital letters are I for the Turkish harmonic vowel set
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        {i y ɯ u
       </span>
       } and D for the conflated flapped middle consonant of American English
       <i>
        writer
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        rider
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       V, F and C have different meanings as
       <a href="/wiki/Voice_Quality_Symbols" title="Voice Quality Symbols">
        Voice Quality Symbols
       </a>
       , where they stand for 'voice', 'falsetto' and 'creak'. They may take diacritics that indicate what kind of voice quality an utterance has, and may be used to extract a suprasegmental feature that occurs on all susceptible segments in a stretch of IPA. For instance, the transcription of
       <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">
        Scottish Gaelic
       </a>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [kʷʰuˣʷt̪ʷs̟ʷ]
       </span>
       'cat' and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [kʷʰʉˣʷt͜ʃʷ]
       </span>
       'cats' (
       <a href="/wiki/Islay" title="Islay">
        Islay
       </a>
       dialect) can be made more economical by extracting the suprasegmental labialization of the words:
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        Vʷ[kʰuˣt̪s̟]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        Vʷ[kʰʉˣt͜ʃ]
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Typography_and_iconicity">
        Typography and iconicity
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Typography and iconicity">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The International Phonetic Alphabet is based on the Latin alphabet, using as few non-Latin forms as possible.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPA194-196_7-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-IPA194-196-7">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Association created the IPA so that the sound values of most
       <a href="/wiki/Consonant" title="Consonant">
        consonant
       </a>
       letters taken from the Latin alphabet would correspond to "international usage".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPA194-196_7-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-IPA194-196-7">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Hence, the letters ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        b
       </span>
       ⟩, ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        d
       </span>
       ⟩, ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        f
       </span>
       ⟩, (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hard_G" title="Hard G">
        hard
       </a>
       ) ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɡ
       </span>
       ⟩, (non-silent) ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        h
       </span>
       ⟩, (unaspirated) ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        k
       </span>
       ⟩, ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        l
       </span>
       ⟩, ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        m
       </span>
       ⟩, ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        n
       </span>
       ⟩, (unaspirated) ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        p
       </span>
       ⟩, (voiceless) ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        s
       </span>
       ⟩, (unaspirated) ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        t
       </span>
       ⟩, ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        v
       </span>
       ⟩, ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        w
       </span>
       ⟩, and ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        z
       </span>
       ⟩ have the values used in English; and the
       <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">
        vowel
       </a>
       letters from the Latin alphabet (⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        a
       </span>
       ⟩, ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        e
       </span>
       ⟩, ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        i
       </span>
       ⟩, ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        o
       </span>
       ⟩, ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        u
       </span>
       ⟩) correspond to the (long) sound values of Latin:
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [i]
       </span>
       is like the vowel in
       <i>
        mach
        <strong>
         i
        </strong>
        ne
       </i>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [u]
       </span>
       is as in
       <i>
        r
        <strong>
         u
        </strong>
        le
       </i>
       , etc. Other letters may differ from English, but are used with these values in other European languages, such as ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        j
       </span>
       ⟩, ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        r
       </span>
       ⟩, and ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        y
       </span>
       ⟩.
      </p>
      <p>
       This inventory was extended by using small-capital and cursive forms, diacritics and rotation. There are also several symbols derived or taken from the Greek alphabet, though the sound values may differ. For example, ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ʋ
       </span>
       ⟩ is a vowel in Greek, but an only indirectly related consonant in the IPA. For most of these, subtly different
       <a href="/wiki/Glyph" title="Glyph">
        glyph
       </a>
       shapes have been devised for the IPA, namely ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɑ
       </span>
       ⟩, ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ꞵ
       </span>
       ⟩, ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɣ
       </span>
       ⟩, ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɛ
       </span>
       ⟩, ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɸ
       </span>
       ⟩, ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ꭓ
       </span>
       ⟩, and ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ʋ
       </span>
       ⟩, which are encoded in
       <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">
        Unicode
       </a>
       separately from their parent Greek letters, though one of them – ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        θ
       </span>
       ⟩ – is not, while Greek ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        β
       </span>
       ⟩ and ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        χ
       </span>
       ⟩ are generally used for ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ꞵ
       </span>
       ⟩ and ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ꭓ
       </span>
       ⟩.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The sound values of modified Latin letters can often be derived from those of the original letters.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       For example, letters with a rightward-facing hook at the bottom represent
       <a href="/wiki/Retroflex_consonant" title="Retroflex consonant">
        retroflex consonants
       </a>
       ; and small capital letters usually represent
       <a href="/wiki/Uvular_consonant" title="Uvular consonant">
        uvular consonants
       </a>
       . Apart from the fact that certain kinds of modification to the shape of a letter generally correspond to certain kinds of modification to the sound represented, there is no way to deduce the sound represented by a symbol from its shape (as for example in
       <a href="/wiki/Visible_Speech" title="Visible Speech">
        Visible Speech
       </a>
       ) nor even any systematic relation between signs and the sounds they represent (as in
       <a href="/wiki/Hangul" title="Hangul">
        Hangul
       </a>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       Beyond the letters themselves, there are a variety of secondary symbols which aid in transcription.
       <a href="/wiki/Diacritic" title="Diacritic">
        Diacritic marks
       </a>
       can be combined with IPA letters to transcribe modified
       <a href="/wiki/Phonetics" title="Phonetics">
        phonetic
       </a>
       values or
       <a href="/wiki/Secondary_articulation" title="Secondary articulation">
        secondary articulations
       </a>
       . There are also special symbols for
       <a href="/wiki/Prosody_(linguistics)" title="Prosody (linguistics)">
        suprasegmental features
       </a>
       such as
       <a href="/wiki/Stress_(linguistics)" title="Stress (linguistics)">
        stress
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Tone_(linguistics)" title="Tone (linguistics)">
        tone
       </a>
       that are often employed.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Types_of_transcription">
        Types of transcription
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Types of transcription">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       There are two principal types of
       <a href="/wiki/Bracket" title="Bracket">
        brackets
       </a>
       used to set off IPA transcriptions:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        [square brackets] are used with
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phonetic" title="Phonetic">
         phonetic
        </a>
        notations, possibly including details of the pronunciation that may not be used for distinguishing words in the language being transcribed, but which the author nonetheless wishes to document.
       </li>
       <li>
        /
        <a href="/wiki/Slash_(punctuation)" title="Slash (punctuation)">
         slashes
        </a>
        / are used for
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phonemic" title="Phonemic">
         phonemic
        </a>
        notations, which note only features that are distinctive in the language, without any extraneous detail.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       For example, while the
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /p/
       </span>
       sounds of
       <i>
        pin
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        spin
       </i>
       are pronounced slightly differently in English (and this difference would be meaningful in some languages), the difference is not meaningful in English. Thus
       <i>
        phonemically
       </i>
       the words are
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /pɪn/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /spɪn/
       </span>
       , with the same
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /p/
       </span>
       phoneme. However, to capture the difference between them (the
       <a href="/wiki/Allophone" title="Allophone">
        allophones
       </a>
       of
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /p/
       </span>
       ), they can be transcribed phonetically as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [pʰɪn]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [spɪn]
       </span>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Other conventions are less commonly seen:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Double slashes //...//, pipes |...|, double pipes ||...||, or braces {...} may be used around a word to denote its underlying structure, more abstract even than that of phonemes.
        <span class="plainlinks selfreference" style="font-style: italic;">
         See
         <a href="/wiki/Morphophonology" title="Morphophonology">
          morphophonology
         </a>
         for examples.
        </span>
       </li>
       <li>
        Double square brackets
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         ⟦...⟧
        </span>
        are used for extra-precise transcription. They indicate that a letter has its cardinal IPA value. For example,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         ⟦a⟧
        </span>
        is an open front vowel, rather than the perhaps slightly different value (such as open central) that "
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [a]
        </span>
        " may be used to transcribe in a particular language. Thus two vowels transcribed for easy legibility as ⟨
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [e]
        </span>
        ⟩ and ⟨
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ɛ]
        </span>
        ⟩ may be clarified as actually being
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         ⟦e̝⟧
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         ⟦e⟧
        </span>
        ; ⟨
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ð]
        </span>
        ⟩ may be more precisely
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         ⟦ð̠̞ˠ⟧
        </span>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
         <a href="#cite_note-23">
          [19]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Angle brackets are used to clarify that the letters represent the original orthography of the language, or sometimes an exact transliteration of a non-Latin script, not the IPA; or, within the IPA, that the letters themselves are indicated, not the sound values that they carry. For example, ⟨
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         pin
        </span>
        ⟩ and ⟨
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         spin
        </span>
        ⟩ would be seen for those words, which do not contain the
        <i>
         ee
        </i>
        sound
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [i]
        </span>
        of the IPA letter ⟨
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         i
        </span>
        ⟩. Italics are perhaps more commonly used for this purpose when full words are being written (as
        <i>
         pin, spin
        </i>
        above), but may not be sufficiently clear for individual letters and digraphs.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
         <a href="#cite_note-24">
          [20]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        {
        <a href="/wiki/Bracket#Braces_or_curly_brackets_.7B_.7D" title="Bracket">
         Braces
        </a>
        } are used for
        <a href="/wiki/Prosody_(linguistics)" title="Prosody (linguistics)">
         prosodic
        </a>
        notation. See
        <a href="/wiki/Extensions_to_the_International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="Extensions to the International Phonetic Alphabet">
         Extensions to the International Phonetic Alphabet
        </a>
        for examples in that system.
       </li>
       <li>
        (Parentheses) are used for indistinguishable utterances. They are also seen for silent articulation (mouthing), where the expected phonetic transcription is derived from lip-reading, and with periods to indicate silent pauses, for example (...).
       </li>
       <li>
        Double parentheses indicate obscured or unintelligible sound, as in ((2 syll.)) or ⸨2 syll.⸩, two audible but unidentifiable syllables.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Handwritten_forms">
        Handwritten forms
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Handwritten forms">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cursive_IPA_open_o.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="101" data-file-width="724" height="56" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Cursive_IPA_open_o.png/400px-Cursive_IPA_open_o.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Cursive_IPA_open_o.png/600px-Cursive_IPA_open_o.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/79/Cursive_IPA_open_o.png 2x" width="400"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cursive_IPA_open_o.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         An example of a printed text with IPA letters filled in by hand. The two words at the beginning of line 1 are
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          sɨk
         </span>
         and
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          sɔ̄k
         </span>
         . The
         <i>
          ɔ
         </i>
         has a cursive form that looks somewhat like a
         <i>
          2
         </i>
         or a small-capital
         <i>
          Q
         </i>
         in some cursive hands.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Handwritten_IPA" title="Handwritten IPA">
        Handwritten IPA
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       IPA letters have
       <a href="/wiki/Handwriting" title="Handwriting">
        handwritten
       </a>
       forms designed for use in manuscripts and when taking field notes; they are occasionally seen in publications when the printer did not have fonts that supported IPA, and the IPA was therefore filled in by hand.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Letter_g">
        Letter g
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Letter g">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LowercaseG.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="468" data-file-width="396" height="260" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/LowercaseG.svg/220px-LowercaseG.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/LowercaseG.svg/330px-LowercaseG.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/LowercaseG.svg/440px-LowercaseG.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LowercaseG.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Typographic variants include a double-story and single-story
         <b>
          g
         </b>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The 1949
       <i>
        Principles of the
        <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Association" title="International Phonetic Association">
         International Phonetic Association
        </a>
       </i>
       recommends using
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Opentail_g.svg">
        <img alt="Opentail g.svg" data-file-height="196" data-file-width="115" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Opentail_g.svg/8px-Opentail_g.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Opentail_g.svg/12px-Opentail_g.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Opentail_g.svg/16px-Opentail_g.svg.png 2x" width="8"/>
       </a>
       (open tail g) for advanced
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_plosive" title="Voiced velar plosive">
        voiced velar plosives
       </a>
       (denoted by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C9%A1" title="ɡ">
        Latin small letter script G
       </a>
       ) and
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Looptail_g.svg">
        <img alt="Looptail g.svg" data-file-height="196" data-file-width="132" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Looptail_g.svg/8px-Looptail_g.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Looptail_g.svg/12px-Looptail_g.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Looptail_g.svg/16px-Looptail_g.svg.png 2x" width="8"/>
       </a>
       (loop tail g) for regular ones where the two are contrasted, but this suggestion was never accepted by
       <a href="/wiki/Phonetics" title="Phonetics">
        phoneticians
       </a>
       in general,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-symbolguide_25-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-symbolguide-25">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and today '
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Opentail_g.svg">
        <img alt="Opentail g.svg" data-file-height="196" data-file-width="115" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Opentail_g.svg/8px-Opentail_g.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Opentail_g.svg/12px-Opentail_g.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Opentail_g.svg/16px-Opentail_g.svg.png 2x" width="8"/>
       </a>
       ' is the symbol used in the
       <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">
        International Phonetic Alphabet
       </a>
       , with '
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Looptail_g.svg">
        <img alt="Looptail g.svg" data-file-height="196" data-file-width="132" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Looptail_g.svg/8px-Looptail_g.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Looptail_g.svg/12px-Looptail_g.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Looptail_g.svg/16px-Looptail_g.svg.png 2x" width="8"/>
       </a>
       ' acknowledged as an acceptable variant and more often used in printed materials.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-symbolguide_25-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-symbolguide-25">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Modifying_the_IPA_chart">
        <span id="chart">
        </span>
        Modifying the IPA chart
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Modifying the IPA chart">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:IPA_chart_2005.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2144" data-file-width="1656" height="388" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/IPA_chart_2005.png/300px-IPA_chart_2005.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/IPA_chart_2005.png/450px-IPA_chart_2005.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/IPA_chart_2005.png/600px-IPA_chart_2005.png 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:IPA_chart_2005.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The authors of textbooks or similar publications often create revised versions of the IPA chart to express their own preferences or needs. The image displays one such version. Only the black symbols are part of the IPA; common additional symbols are in grey.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The International Phonetic Alphabet is occasionally modified by the Association. After each modification, the Association provides an updated simplified presentation of the alphabet in the form of a chart. (See
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="History of the International Phonetic Alphabet">
        History of the IPA
       </a>
       .) Not all aspects of the alphabet can be accommodated in a chart of the size published by the IPA. The
       <a href="/wiki/Alveolo-palatal_consonant" title="Alveolo-palatal consonant">
        alveolo-palatal
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epiglottal_consonant" title="Epiglottal consonant">
        epiglottal
       </a>
       consonants, for example, are not included in the consonant chart for reasons of space rather than of theory (two additional columns would be required, one between the retroflex and palatal columns and the other between the pharyngeal and glottal columns), and the
       <a href="/wiki/Lateral_flap" title="Lateral flap">
        lateral flap
       </a>
       would require an additional row for that single consonant, so they are listed instead under the catchall block of "other symbols".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The indefinitely large number of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chao_tone_letter" title="Chao tone letter">
        tone letters
       </a>
       would make a full accounting impractical even on a larger page, and only a few examples are shown.
      </p>
      <p>
       The procedure for modifying the alphabet or the chart is to propose the change in the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Journal_of_the_International_Phonetic_Association" title="Journal of the International Phonetic Association">
         Journal of the IPA
        </a>
        .
       </i>
       (See, for example, August 2008 on a
       <a href="/wiki/Open_central_vowel" title="Open central vowel">
        open central vowel
       </a>
       and August 2011 on central approximants.)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cambridge1_27-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-cambridge1-27">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Reactions to the proposal may be published in the same or subsequent issues of the Journal (as in August 2009 on the open central vowel).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A formal proposal is then put to the Council of the IPA
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       – which is elected by the membership
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       – for further discussion and a formal vote.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Only changes to the alphabet or chart that have been approved by the Council can be considered part of the official IPA. Nonetheless, many users of the alphabet, including the leadership of the Association itself, make personal changes or additions in their own practice, either for convenience in working on a particular language (see "Illustrations of the IPA" for individual languages in the
       <i>
        Handbook
       </i>
       , which for example may use ⟨c⟩ for
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [tʃ]
       </span>
       ),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       or because they object to some aspect of the official version.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Usage">
        Usage
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Usage">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a href="/wiki/Phonetic_transcription" title="Phonetic transcription">
        Phonetic transcription
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Although the IPA offers over 160 symbols for transcribing speech, only a relatively small subset of these will be used to transcribe any one language. It is possible to transcribe speech with various levels of precision. A precise phonetic transcription, in which sounds are described in a great deal of detail, is known as a
       <i>
        narrow transcription
       </i>
       . A coarser transcription which ignores some of this detail is called a
       <i>
        broad transcription.
       </i>
       Both are relative terms, and both are generally enclosed in square brackets.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPA_1999_34-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-IPA_1999-34">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Broad phonetic transcriptions may restrict themselves to easily heard details, or only to details that are relevant to the discussion at hand, and may differ little if at all from phonemic transcriptions, but they make no theoretical claim that all the distinctions transcribed are necessarily meaningful in the language.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RPGA_international.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="280" data-file-width="670" height="84" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/RPGA_international.svg/200px-RPGA_international.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/RPGA_international.svg/300px-RPGA_international.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/RPGA_international.svg/400px-RPGA_international.svg.png 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RPGA_international.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Phonetic transcriptions of the word
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/International" title="International">
           international
          </a>
         </i>
         in two
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_dialect" title="English dialect">
          English dialects
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       For example, the English word
       <i>
        little
       </i>
       may be transcribed broadly using the IPA as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˈlɪtəl]
       </span>
       , and this broad (imprecise) transcription is a more or less accurate description of many pronunciations. A narrower transcription may focus on individual or dialectical details:
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˈɫɪɾɫ]
       </span>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/General_American" title="General American">
        General American
       </a>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˈlɪʔo]
       </span>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Cockney" title="Cockney">
        Cockney
       </a>
       , or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˈɫɪːɫ]
       </span>
       in Southern US English.
      </p>
      <p>
       It is customary to use simpler letters, without many diacritics, in phonemic transcriptions. The choice of IPA letters may reflect the theoretical claims of the author, or merely be a convenience for typesetting. For instance, in English, either the vowel of
       <i>
        pick
       </i>
       or the vowel of
       <i>
        peak
       </i>
       may be transcribed as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /i/
       </span>
       (for the pairs
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /pik, piːk/
       </span>
       or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /pɪk, pik/
       </span>
       ), and neither is identical to the vowel of the French word
       <i>
        <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr">
         pique
        </span>
       </i>
       which is also generally transcribed
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /i/
       </span>
       . That is, letters between slashes do not have absolute values, something true of broader phonetic approximations as well. A narrow transcription may, however, be used to distinguish them:
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [pʰɪk]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [pʰiːk]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [pikʲ]
       </span>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Linguists">
        Linguists
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Linguists">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Although IPA is popular for transcription by linguists, American linguists often alternate use of the IPA with
       <a href="/wiki/Americanist_phonetic_notation" title="Americanist phonetic notation">
        Americanist phonetic notation
       </a>
       or use the IPA together with some
       <a href="/wiki/Obsolete_and_nonstandard_symbols_in_the_International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="Obsolete and nonstandard symbols in the International Phonetic Alphabet">
        nonstandard symbols
       </a>
       , for reasons including reducing the error rate on reading handwritten transcriptions or avoiding perceived awkwardness of IPA in some situations. The exact practice may vary somewhat between languages and even individual researchers, so authors are generally encouraged to include a chart or other explanation of their choices.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomason_35-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-thomason-35">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Language_study">
        Language study
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Language study">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg">
          <img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/>
         </a>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text">
         <div class="mbox-text-span">
          <b>
           This section needs expansion
          </b>
          .
          <small>
           You can help by
           <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">
            adding to it
           </a>
           .
          </small>

            (November 2009)
           </i>
          </small>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
