      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:274px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:English-2nd_grade-Vereshchagina-Russian-textbook-page_39.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="366" data-file-width="272" height="366" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/d/d7/English-2nd_grade-Vereshchagina-Russian-textbook-page_39.png" width="272"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:English-2nd_grade-Vereshchagina-Russian-textbook-page_39.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A page from an English language textbook used in
         <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">
          Russia
         </a>
         . The IPA is used to teach the different pronunciations of the
         <a href="/wiki/Th_(digraph)" title="Th (digraph)">
          digraph ⟨th⟩
         </a>
         (
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /θ/
         </span>
         ,
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ð/
         </span>
         ) and to show the pronunciation of newly introduced words
         <i>
          polite
         </i>
         ,
         <i>
          everything
         </i>
         ,
         <i>
          always
         </i>
         ,
         <i>
          forget
         </i>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Some language study programs use the IPA to teach pronunciation. For example, in Russia (and earlier in the Soviet Union) and
       <a href="/wiki/Mainland_China" title="Mainland China">
        mainland China
       </a>
       , textbooks for children
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and adults
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       for studying English and French consistently use the IPA. English teachers and textbooks in Taiwan tend to use the Kenyon and Knott system, a slight typographical variant of the IPA first used in the 1944
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/A_Pronouncing_Dictionary_of_American_English" title="A Pronouncing Dictionary of American English">
         Pronouncing Dictionary of American English
        </a>
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Dictionaries">
        Dictionaries
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Dictionaries">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="English">
        English
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: English">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Many British dictionaries, including the
       <a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">
        Oxford English Dictionary
       </a>
       and some
       <a href="/wiki/Monolingual_learner%27s_dictionary" title="Monolingual learner's dictionary">
        learner's dictionaries
       </a>
       such as the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Oxford_Advanced_Learner%27s_Dictionary" title="Oxford Advanced Learner's Dictionary">
         Oxford Advanced Learner's Dictionary
        </a>
       </i>
       and the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_Advanced_Learner%27s_Dictionary" title="Cambridge Advanced Learner's Dictionary">
         Cambridge Advanced Learner's Dictionary
        </a>
       </i>
       , now use
       <a href="/wiki/Pronunciation_respelling_for_English#International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="Pronunciation respelling for English">
        the International Phonetic Alphabet
       </a>
       to represent the pronunciation of words.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, most American (and some British) volumes use one of a variety of
       <a href="/wiki/Pronunciation_respelling_for_English" title="Pronunciation respelling for English">
        pronunciation respelling
       </a>
       systems, intended to be more comfortable for readers of English. For example, the respelling systems in many American dictionaries (such as
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merriam-Webster_Dictionary" title="Merriam-Webster Dictionary">
         Merriam-Webster
        </a>
       </i>
       ) use ⟨y⟩ for IPA
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [j]
       </span>
       and ⟨sh⟩ for IPA
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ʃ]
       </span>
       , reflecting common representations of those sounds in written English,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       using only letters of the English
       <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">
        Roman alphabet
       </a>
       and variations of them. (In IPA,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [y]
       </span>
       represents the sound of the French ⟨u⟩ (as in
       <i>
        <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr">
         tu
        </span>
       </i>
       ), and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [sh]
       </span>
       represents the pair of sounds in
       <i>
        gra
        <strong>
         ssh
        </strong>
        opper
       </i>
       .)
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_languages">
        Other languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Other languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The IPA is also not universal among dictionaries in languages other than English. Monolingual dictionaries of languages with generally
       <a href="/wiki/Phonemic_orthography" title="Phonemic orthography">
        phonemic orthographies
       </a>
       generally do not bother with indicating the pronunciation of most words, and tend to use respelling systems for words with unexpected pronunciations. Dictionaries produced in
       <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">
        Israel
       </a>
       use the IPA rarely and sometimes use the
       <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_alphabet" title="Hebrew alphabet">
        Hebrew alphabet
       </a>
       for transcription of foreign words. Monolingual Hebrew dictionaries use pronunciation respelling for words with unusual spelling; for example, the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Even-Shoshan_Dictionary" title="Even-Shoshan Dictionary">
         Even-Shoshan Dictionary
        </a>
       </i>
       respells
       <span class="script-hebrew" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 115%; font-family: 'Ezra SIL', 'Ezra SIL SR', 'Keter Aram Tsova', 'Taamey Ashkenaz', 'Taamey David CLM', 'Taamey Frank CLM', 'Frenk Ruehl CLM', 'Keter YG', 'Shofar', 'David CLM', 'Hadasim CLM', 'Simple CLM', 'Nachlieli', 'SBL BibLit', 'SBL Hebrew', Cardo, Alef, 'Noto Serif Hebrew', 'Noto Sans Hebrew', 'David Libre', David, 'Times New Roman', Gisha, Arial, FreeSerif, FreeSans;">
        תָּכְנִית
       </span>
       ‬ as
       <span class="script-hebrew" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 115%; font-family: 'Ezra SIL', 'Ezra SIL SR', 'Keter Aram Tsova', 'Taamey Ashkenaz', 'Taamey David CLM', 'Taamey Frank CLM', 'Frenk Ruehl CLM', 'Keter YG', 'Shofar', 'David CLM', 'Hadasim CLM', 'Simple CLM', 'Nachlieli', 'SBL BibLit', 'SBL Hebrew', Cardo, Alef, 'Noto Serif Hebrew', 'Noto Sans Hebrew', 'David Libre', David, 'Times New Roman', Gisha, Arial, FreeSerif, FreeSans;">
        תּוֹכְנִית
       </span>
       ‬ because this word uses
       <a href="/wiki/Kamatz" title="Kamatz">
        kamatz
       </a>
       katan. Bilingual dictionaries that translate from foreign languages into
       <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">
        Russian
       </a>
       usually employ the IPA, but monolingual Russian dictionaries occasionally use pronunciation respelling for foreign words; for example,
       <a href="/wiki/Sergey_Ozhegov" title="Sergey Ozhegov">
        Sergey Ozhegov
       </a>
       's dictionary adds нэ́ in brackets for the
       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
        French
       </a>
       word пенсне (
       <i>
        <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr">
         <a href="/wiki/Pince-nez" title="Pince-nez">
          pince-nez
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       ) to indicate that the final
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%D0%95" title="Е">
        е
       </a>
       does not
       <a href="/wiki/Iotation" title="Iotation">
        iotate
       </a>
       the preceding
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%D0%9D" title="Н">
        н
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The IPA is more common in bilingual dictionaries, but there are exceptions here too. Mass-market bilingual Czech dictionaries, for instance, tend to use the IPA only for sounds not found in the
       <a href="/wiki/Czech_language" title="Czech language">
        Czech language
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Standard_orthographies_and_case_variants">
        Standard orthographies and case variants
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Standard orthographies and case variants">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Case_variants_of_IPA_letters" title="Case variants of IPA letters">
        Case variants of IPA letters
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       IPA letters have been incorporated into the alphabets of various languages, notably via the
       <a href="/wiki/Africa_Alphabet" title="Africa Alphabet">
        Africa Alphabet
       </a>
       in many sub-Saharan languages such as
       <a href="/wiki/Hausa_language" title="Hausa language">
        Hausa
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Fula_language" title="Fula language">
        Fula
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Akan_language" title="Akan language">
        Akan
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Gbe_languages" title="Gbe languages">
        Gbe languages
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Manding_languages" title="Manding languages">
        Manding languages
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lingala_language" title="Lingala language">
        Lingala
       </a>
       , etc. This has created the need for capital variants. For example,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kabiy%C3%A9_language" title="Kabiyé language">
        Kabiyè
       </a>
       of northern
       <a href="/wiki/Togo" title="Togo">
        Togo
       </a>
       has
       <a href="/wiki/African_D" title="African D">
        Ɖ ɖ
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Eng_(letter)" title="Eng (letter)">
        Ŋ ŋ
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_gamma" title="Latin gamma">
        Ɣ ɣ
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Open_O" title="Open O">
        Ɔ ɔ
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Latin_epsilon" title="Latin epsilon">
        Ɛ ɛ
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/%C6%B2" title="Ʋ">
        Ʋ ʋ
       </a>
       . These, and others, are supported by
       <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">
        Unicode
       </a>
       , but appear in Latin ranges other than the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IPA_Extensions_(Unicode_block)" title="IPA Extensions (Unicode block)">
        IPA extensions
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In the IPA itself, however, only lower-case letters are used. The 1949 edition of the IPA handbook indicated that an asterisk ⟨*⟩ may be prefixed to indicate that a word is a proper name,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but this convention has not been included in recent editions.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_singing">
        Classical singing
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Classical singing">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       IPA has widespread use among classical singers for preparation, especially among English-speaking singers who are expected to sing in a variety of foreign languages. Opera
       <a href="/wiki/Libretto" title="Libretto">
        librettos
       </a>
       are authoritatively transcribed in IPA, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Nico_Castel" title="Nico Castel">
        Nico Castel
       </a>
       's volumes
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and Timothy Cheek's book
       <i>
        Singing in Czech
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Opera singers' ability to read IPA was used by the site
       <i>
        Visual Thesaurus
       </i>
       , which employed several opera singers "to make recordings for the 150,000 words and phrases in VT's lexical database. ...for their vocal stamina, attention to the details of enunciation, and most of all, knowledge of IPA."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Letters">
        Letters
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Letters">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet_chart" title="International Phonetic Alphabet chart">
        International Phonetic Alphabet chart
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The International Phonetic Association organizes the letters of the IPA into three categories:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pulmonic_sounds" title="Pulmonic sounds">
        pulmonic
       </a>
       consonants, non-pulmonic consonants, and vowels.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Pulmonic consonant letters are arranged singly or in pairs of voiceless (
       <a href="/wiki/Tenuis_consonant" title="Tenuis consonant">
        tenuis
       </a>
       ) and voiced sounds, with these then grouped in columns from front (labial) sounds on the left to back (glottal) sounds on the right. In official publications by the IPA,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       two columns are omitted to save space, with the letters listed among 'other symbols',
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and with the remaining consonants arranged in rows from full closure (occlusives: stops and nasals), to brief closure (vibrants: trills and taps), to partial closure (fricatives) and minimal closure (approximants), again with a row left out to save space. In the table below, a slightly different arrangement is made: All pulmonic consonants are included in the pulmonic-consonant table, and the vibrants and laterals are separated out so that the rows reflect the common
       <a href="/wiki/Lenition" title="Lenition">
        lenition
       </a>
       pathway of
       <i>
        stop → fricative → approximant,
       </i>
       as well as the fact that several letters pull double duty as both fricative and approximant;
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Affricate" title="Affricate">
        affricates
       </a>
       may be created by joining stops and fricatives from adjacent cells. Shaded cells are judged to be implausible.
      </p>
      <p>
       Vowel letters are also grouped in pairs—of unrounded and rounded vowel sounds—with these pairs also arranged from front on the left to back on the right, and from maximal closure at top to minimal closure at bottom. No vowel letters are omitted from the chart, though in the past some of the mid central vowels were listed among the 'other symbols'.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="IPA_number">
        IPA number
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: IPA number">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Each character is assigned a number, to prevent confusion between similar characters (such as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɵ
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        θ
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɤ
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɣ
       </span>
       , or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ʃ
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ʄ
       </span>
       ) in such situations as the printing of manuscripts. The categories of sounds are assigned different ranges of numbers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The numbers are assigned to sounds and to symbols, e.g. 304 is the
       <a href="/wiki/Open_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Open front unrounded vowel">
        open front unrounded vowel
       </a>
       , 415 is the centralization diacritic. Together, they form a symbol that represents the
       <a href="/wiki/Open_central_unrounded_vowel" title="Open central unrounded vowel">
        open central unrounded vowel
       </a>
       . There is no three digit number for the open central unrounded vowel.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Consonants">
        Consonants
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Consonants">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Consonant" title="Consonant">
        Consonant
       </a>
      </div>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Pulmonic_consonants">
        Pulmonic consonants
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Pulmonic consonants">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       A
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egressive" title="Egressive">
        pulmonic
       </a>
       consonant is a consonant made by obstructing the
       <a href="/wiki/Glottis" title="Glottis">
        glottis
       </a>
       (the space between the vocal cords) or
       <a href="/wiki/Human_mouth" title="Human mouth">
        oral cavity
       </a>
       (the mouth) and either simultaneously or subsequently letting out air from the lungs. Pulmonic consonants make up the majority of consonants in the IPA, as well as in human language. All consonants in the English language fall into this category.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The pulmonic consonant table, which includes most consonants, is arranged in rows that designate
       <a href="/wiki/Manner_of_articulation" title="Manner of articulation">
        manner of articulation
       </a>
       , meaning how the consonant is produced, and columns that designate
       <a href="/wiki/Place_of_articulation" title="Place of articulation">
        place of articulation
       </a>
       , meaning where in the vocal tract the consonant is produced. The main chart includes only consonants with a single place of articulation.
      </p>

      </table>
      <p>
       <b>
        Notes
       </b>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        In rows where some letters appear in pairs (the
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Obstruent" title="Obstruent">
          obstruents
         </a>
        </i>
        ), the letter to the right represents a
        <a href="/wiki/Voice_(phonetics)" title="Voice (phonetics)">
         voiced consonant
        </a>
        (except
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Breathy_voice" title="Breathy voice">
         breathy-voiced
        </a>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ɦ]
        </span>
        ). However,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ʔ]
        </span>
        cannot be voiced, and the voicing of
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ʡ]
        </span>
        is ambiguous.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
         <a href="#cite_note-51">
          [47]
         </a>
        </sup>
        In the other rows (the
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Sonorant" title="Sonorant">
          sonorants
         </a>
        </i>
        ), the single letter represents a voiced consonant.
       </li>
       <li>
        Although there is a single letter for the coronal places of articulation for all consonants but fricatives, when dealing with a particular language, the letters may be treated as specifically dental, alveolar, or post-alveolar, as appropriate for that language, without diacritics.
       </li>
       <li>
        Shaded areas indicate articulations judged to be impossible.
       </li>
       <li>
        The letters
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ʁ, ʕ, ʢ]
        </span>
        represent either voiced fricatives or approximants.
       </li>
       <li>
        In many languages, such as English,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [h]
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ɦ]
        </span>
        are not actually glottal, fricatives, or approximants. Rather, they are bare
        <a href="/wiki/Phonation" title="Phonation">
         phonation
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
         <a href="#cite_note-52">
          [48]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        It is primarily the shape of the tongue rather than its position that distinguishes the fricatives
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ʃ ʒ]
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ɕ ʑ]
        </span>
        , and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ʂ ʐ]
        </span>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        Some listed phones are not known to exist as
        <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">
         phonemes
        </a>
        in any language.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Non-pulmonic_consonants">
        Non-pulmonic consonants
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Non-pulmonic consonants">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Non-pulmonic consonants are sounds whose airflow is not dependent on the lungs. These include
       <a href="/wiki/Click_consonant" title="Click consonant">
        clicks
       </a>
       (found in the
       <a href="/wiki/Khoisan_languages" title="Khoisan languages">
        Khoisan languages
       </a>
       of Africa),
       <a href="/wiki/Implosive_consonant" title="Implosive consonant">
        implosives
       </a>
       (found in languages such as
       <a href="/wiki/Sindhi_language" title="Sindhi language">
        Sindhi
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Saraiki_language" title="Saraiki language">
        Saraiki
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Swahili_language" title="Swahili language">
        Swahili
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_language" title="Vietnamese language">
        Vietnamese
       </a>
       ), and
       <a href="/wiki/Ejective_consonant" title="Ejective consonant">
        ejectives
       </a>
       (found in many
       <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_languages_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous languages of the Americas">
        Amerindian
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_the_Caucasus" title="Languages of the Caucasus">
        Caucasian languages
       </a>
       ).
      </p>
      <table class="" style="">
       <tr>

       <tr>
        <td style="text-align: left; font-size: 85%;">
         <div class="hlist navbar" style="float: right;">
          <ul>
           <li>
            <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">
             help
            </a>
           </li>
           <li>
            <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet_chart" title="International Phonetic Alphabet chart">
             full chart
            </a>
           </li>
           <li>
            <a href="/wiki/Template:IPA_non-pulmonic_consonants" title="Template:IPA non-pulmonic consonants">
             template
            </a>
           </li>
          </ul>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       <b>
        Notes
       </b>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Clicks have traditionally been described as double articulation of a forward 'release' and a rear 'accompaniment', with the click letters representing only the release. Therefore, all clicks require two letters for proper notation: ⟨
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         k͡ǂ, ɡ͡ǂ, ŋ͡ǂ, q͡ǂ, ɢ͡ǂ, ɴ͡ǂ
        </span>
        ⟩
        <i>
         etc.
        </i>
        , or ⟨
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         ǂ͡k, ǂ͡ɡ, ǂ͡ŋ, ǂ͡q, ǂ͡ɢ, ǂ͡ɴ
        </span>
        ⟩. When the dorsal articulation is omitted, a
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [k]
        </span>
        may usually be assumed. However, recent research disputes the concept of 'accompaniment' and the idea that clicks are doubly articulated, with the rear occlusion instead simply being part of the airstream mechanisms.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
         <a href="#cite_note-53">
          [49]
         </a>
        </sup>
        In these approaches, the click letter represents both articulations, with the different letters representing different click 'types', there is no velar-uvular distinction, and the accompanying letter represents the manner, phonation, or airstream
        <a href="/wiki/Contour_(linguistics)" title="Contour (linguistics)">
         contour
        </a>
        of the click: ⟨
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         ǂ, ᶢǂ, ᵑǂ
        </span>
        ⟩
        <i>
         etc.
        </i>
       </li>
       <li>
        Letters for the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiceless" title="Voiceless">
         voiceless
        </a>
        implosives ⟨
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         ƥ, ƭ, ƈ, ƙ, ʠ
        </span>
        ⟩ are no longer supported by the IPA, though they remain in Unicode. Instead, the IPA typically uses the voiced equivalent with a voiceless diacritic: ⟨
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         ɓ̥, ʛ̥
        </span>
        ⟩,
        <i>
         etc.
        </i>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        Although not confirmed as contrastive in any language, and therefore not explicitly recognized by the IPA, a letter for the
        <a href="/wiki/Voiced_retroflex_implosive" title="Voiced retroflex implosive">
         retroflex implosive
        </a>
        ,
        <span title="U+1D91">
         ⟨
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          ᶑ
         </span>
         ⟩
        </span>
        , has been assigned an IPA number.
       </li>
       <li>
        The ejective diacritic often stands in for a superscript glottal stop in
        <a href="/wiki/Glottalic_consonant" title="Glottalic consonant">
         glottalized
        </a>
        but pulmonic
        <a href="/wiki/Sonorant" title="Sonorant">
         sonorants
        </a>
        , such as
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [mˀ]
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [lˀ]
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [wˀ]
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [aˀ]
        </span>
        . These may also be transcribed as creaky
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [m̰]
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [l̰]
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [w̰]
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [a̰]
        </span>
        .
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Affricates">
        Affricates
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Affricates">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Affricate_consonant" title="Affricate consonant">
        Affricates
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Doubly_articulated_consonant" title="Doubly articulated consonant">
        co-articulated
       </a>
       stops are represented by two letters joined by a tie bar, either above or below the letters.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The six most common affricates are optionally represented by
       <a href="/wiki/Typographic_ligature" title="Typographic ligature">
        ligatures
       </a>
       , though this is no longer official IPA usage,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPA_1999_34-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-IPA_1999-34">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       because a great number of ligatures would be required to represent all affricates this way. Alternatively, a superscript notation for a consonant release is sometimes used to transcribe affricates, for example
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        tˢ
       </span>
       for
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        t͡s
       </span>
       , paralleling
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        kˣ
       </span>
       ~
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        k͡x
       </span>
       . The letters for the palatal plosives
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        c
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɟ
       </span>
       are often used as a convenience for
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        t͡ʃ
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        d͡ʒ
       </span>
       or similar affricates, even in official IPA publications, so they must be interpreted with care.
      </p>

      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Co-articulated_consonants">
        Co-articulated consonants
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Co-articulated consonants">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Co-articulated_consonant" title="Co-articulated consonant">
        Co-articulated consonants
       </a>
       are sounds that involve two simultaneous
       <a href="/wiki/Place_of_articulation" title="Place of articulation">
        places of articulation
       </a>
       (are pronounced using two parts of the
       <a href="/wiki/Vocal_tract" title="Vocal tract">
        vocal tract
       </a>
       ). In English, the
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [w]
       </span>
       in "went" is a coarticulated consonant, being pronounced by rounding the lips and raising the back of the tongue. Similar sounds are
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ʍ]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɥ]
       </span>
       .
      </p>
      <table class="" style="">
       <tr>
        <td class="nowrap" style="border: 1px solid #a2a9b1; background: #f8f9fa; padding: 0.2em;">

      <p>
       <b>
        Notes
       </b>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ɧ]
        </span>
        is described as a "simultaneous
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ʃ]
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [x]
        </span>
        ".
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
         <a href="#cite_note-55">
          [51]
         </a>
        </sup>
        However, this analysis is disputed. (See
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiceless_palatal-velar_fricative" title="Voiceless palatal-velar fricative">
         voiceless palatal-velar fricative
        </a>
        for discussion.)
       </li>
       <li>
        Multiple tie bars can be used: ⟨
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         a͡b͡c
        </span>
        ⟩ or ⟨
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         a͜b͜c
        </span>
        ⟩. For instance, if a prenasalized stop is transcribed ⟨
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         m͡b
        </span>
        ⟩, and a doubly articulated stop ⟨
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         ɡ͡b
        </span>
        ⟩, then a prenasalized doubly articulated stop would be ⟨
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         ŋ͡m͡ɡ͡b
        </span>
        ⟩
       </li>
       <li>
        On browsers that use
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Arial_Unicode_MS" title="Arial Unicode MS">
          Arial Unicode MS
         </a>
        </i>
        to display IPA characters, the following incorrectly formed sequences may look better due to a bug in that font:
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         kp͡, ɡb͡, ŋm͡
        </span>
        .
       </li>
      </ul>

         ]

         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="400" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Cardinal_vowel_tongue_position-front.svg/200px-Cardinal_vowel_tongue_position-front.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Cardinal_vowel_tongue_position-front.svg/300px-Cardinal_vowel_tongue_position-front.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Cardinal_vowel_tongue_position-front.svg/400px-Cardinal_vowel_tongue_position-front.svg.png 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cardinal_vowel_tongue_position-front.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Tongue positions of
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardinal_vowel" title="Cardinal vowel">
          cardinal
         </a>
         front vowels, with highest point indicated. The position of the highest point is used to determine vowel height and backness.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cardinal_vowels-Jones_x-ray.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="577" data-file-width="588" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Cardinal_vowels-Jones_x-ray.jpg/200px-Cardinal_vowels-Jones_x-ray.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Cardinal_vowels-Jones_x-ray.jpg/300px-Cardinal_vowels-Jones_x-ray.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Cardinal_vowels-Jones_x-ray.jpg/400px-Cardinal_vowels-Jones_x-ray.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cardinal_vowels-Jones_x-ray.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Radiography" title="Radiography">
          X-ray photos
         </a>
         show the sounds
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [i, u, a, ɑ]
         </span>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The IPA defines a vowel as a sound which occurs at a syllable center.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Below is a chart depicting the vowels of the IPA. The IPA maps the vowels according to the position of the tongue.
      </p>

               <div style="position: absolute; left: -0.5em; top: -1.25ex;">

                </div>
                <div style="position: absolute; right: 0em; padding-left: 0.2em; background: #f8f9fa;">

                   ɐ
                  </a>
                 </span>
                </div>
                <div style="position: absolute; left: 1em; padding-right: 0.2em; background: #f8f9fa;">
                 <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
                  <a href="/wiki/Near-open_central_rounded_vowel" title="Near-open central rounded vowel">
                   ɞ̞
                  </a>
                 </span>
                </div>
               </div>
              </div>
              <div style="position: absolute; left: 50%; top: 93%; line-height: 1.2;">
               <div style="position: absolute; left: -0.5em; top: -1.25ex;">

                 <img alt="•" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="512" height="5" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Disc_Plain_black.svg/5px-Disc_Plain_black.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Disc_Plain_black.svg/8px-Disc_Plain_black.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Disc_Plain_black.svg/10px-Disc_Plain_black.svg.png 2x" width="5"/>
                </div>
                <div style="position: absolute; right: 0em; padding-left: 0.2em; background: #f8f9fa;">

                  </a>
                 </span>
                </div>
                <div style="position: absolute; left: 1em; padding-right: 0.2em; background: #f8f9fa;">

                   ä
                  </a>
                 </span>
                </div>

                  </a>

               </div>
              </div>
              <div style="position: absolute; left: 90%; top: 93%; line-height: 1.2;">

                </div>

          <tr>
           <td style="text-align: right; vertical-align: middle; font-size: 85%;">
            <a href="/wiki/Near-close_vowel" title="Near-close vowel">
             Near-close
            </a>
           </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
           <td style="text-align: right; vertical-align: middle; font-size: 85%;">
            <a href="/wiki/Close-mid_vowel" title="Close-mid vowel">
             Close-mid
            </a>
           </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
           <td style="text-align: right; vertical-align: middle; font-size: 85%;">

              Mid
             </a>
            </b>
           </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
           <td style="text-align: right; vertical-align: middle; font-size: 85%;">
            <a href="/wiki/Open-mid_vowel" title="Open-mid vowel">
             Open-mid
            </a>
           </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
           <td style="text-align: right; vertical-align: middle; font-size: 85%;">
            <a href="/wiki/Near-open_vowel" title="Near-open vowel">
             Near-open
            </a>
           </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
           <td style="text-align: right; vertical-align: middle; font-size: 85%;">
            <b>
             <a href="/wiki/Open_vowel" title="Open vowel">
              Open
             </a>
            </b>
           </td>
          </tr>
         </table>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td style="text-align: left; font-size: 85%;">
         <div class="hlist navbar" style="float: right;">
          <ul>
           <li>
            <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">
             help
            </a>
           </li>
           <li>
            <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet_chart" title="International Phonetic Alphabet chart">
             full chart
            </a>
           </li>
           <li>
            <a href="/wiki/Template:IPA_vowels" title="Template:IPA vowels">
             template
            </a>
           </li>
          </ul>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       The vertical axis of the chart is mapped by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vowel_height" title="Vowel height">
        vowel height
       </a>
       . Vowels pronounced with the tongue lowered are at the bottom, and vowels pronounced with the tongue raised are at the top. For example,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɑ]
       </span>
       (the first vowel in
       <i>
        father
       </i>
       ) is at the bottom because the tongue is lowered in this position. However,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [i]
       </span>
       (the vowel in "meet") is at the top because the sound is said with the tongue raised to the roof of the mouth.
      </p>
      <p>
       In a similar fashion, the horizontal axis of the chart is determined by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vowel_backness" title="Vowel backness">
        vowel backness
       </a>
       . Vowels with the tongue moved towards the front of the mouth (such as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɛ]
       </span>
       , the vowel in "met") are to the left in the chart, while those in which it is moved to the back (such as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ʌ]
       </span>
       , the vowel in "but") are placed to the right in the chart.
      </p>
      <p>
       In places where vowels are paired, the right represents a
       <a href="/wiki/Roundedness" title="Roundedness">
        rounded vowel
       </a>
       (in which the lips are rounded) while the left is its unrounded counterpart.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Diphthongs">
        Diphthongs
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Diphthongs">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">
        Diphthongs
       </a>
       are typically specified with a non-syllabic diacritic, as in ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        uɪ̯
       </span>
       ⟩ or ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        u̯ɪ
       </span>
       ⟩, or with a superscript for the on- or off-glide, as in ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        uᶦ
       </span>
       ⟩ or ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ᵘɪ
       </span>
       ⟩. Sometimes a tie bar is used, especially if it is difficult to tell if the diphthong is characterized by an on-glide, an off-glide or is variable: ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        u͡ɪ
       </span>
       ⟩.
      </p>
      <p>
       <b>
        Notes
       </b>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        ⟨
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         a
        </span>
        ⟩ officially represents a front vowel, but there is little distinction between front and central open vowels, and ⟨
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         a
        </span>
        ⟩ is frequently used for an open central vowel.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomason_35-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-thomason-35">
          [31]
         </a>
        </sup>
        However, if disambiguation is required, the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retraction_(phonetics)" title="Retraction (phonetics)">
         retraction diacritic
        </a>
        or the
        <a href="/wiki/Relative_articulation#Centralized_vowels" title="Relative articulation">
         centralized diacritic
        </a>
        may be added to indicate an open central vowel, as in ⟨
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         a̠
        </span>
        ⟩ or ⟨
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         ä
        </span>
        ⟩.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Diacritics_and_prosodic_notation">
        Diacritics and prosodic notation
        <span id="Diacritics">
        </span>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Diacritics and prosodic notation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Diacritic" title="Diacritic">
        Diacritics
       </a>
       are used for phonetic detail. They are added to IPA letters to indicate a modification or specification of that letter's normal pronunciation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPA15_57-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-IPA15-57">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       By being made superscript, any IPA letter may function as a diacritic, conferring elements of its articulation to the base letter. (See
       <a href="/wiki/Secondary_articulation" title="Secondary articulation">
        secondary articulation
       </a>
       for a list of superscript IPA letters supported by Unicode.) Those superscript letters listed below are specifically provided for by the IPA; others include ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        tˢ
       </span>
       ⟩ (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [t]
       </span>
       with fricative release), ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ᵗs
       </span>
       ⟩ (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [s]
       </span>
       with affricate onset), ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ⁿd
       </span>
       ⟩ (prenasalized
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [d]
       </span>
       ), ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        bʱ
       </span>
       ⟩ (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [b]
       </span>
       with breathy voice), ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        mˀ
       </span>
       ⟩ (glottalized
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [m]
       </span>
       ), ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        sᶴ
       </span>
       ⟩ (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [s]
       </span>
       with a flavor of
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ʃ]
       </span>
       ), ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        oᶷ
       </span>
       ⟩ (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [o]
       </span>
       with
       <a href="/wiki/Vowel_breaking" title="Vowel breaking">
        diphthongization
       </a>
       ), ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɯᵝ
       </span>
       ⟩ (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compressed_vowel" title="Compressed vowel">
        compressed
       </a>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɯ]
       </span>
       ). Superscript diacritics placed after a letter are ambiguous between simultaneous modification of the sound and phonetic detail at the end of the sound. For example, labialized ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        kʷ
       </span>
       ⟩ may mean either simultaneous
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [k]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [w]
       </span>
       or else
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [k]
       </span>
       with a labialized release. Superscript diacritics placed before a letter, on the other hand, normally indicate a modification of the onset of the sound (⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        mˀ
       </span>
       ⟩ glottalized
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [m]
       </span>
       , ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ˀm
       </span>
       ⟩
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [m]
       </span>
       with a glottal onset).
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th colspan="6">

        </th>
       </tr>

        </td>

         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          ɪ̯ ʊ̯
         </span>
        </td>
        <td rowspan="2">
         <a href="/wiki/Semivowel" title="Semivowel">
          Non-syllabic
         </a>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td style="text-align:center; font-size:xx-large; line-height:1em;">
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          ◌̍
         </span>
        </td>
        <td style="text-align:center; font-size:larger;">
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          ɻ̍ ŋ̍
         </span>
        </td>
        <td style="text-align:center; font-size:xx-large; line-height:1em;">
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          ◌̑
         </span>

       <b>
        Notes
       </b>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Aspirated">
         <b>
          <a href="#ref_Aspirated">
           ^a
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        With aspirated voiced consonants, the aspiration is usually also voiced (voiced aspirated – but see
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aspirated_voiced" title="Aspirated voiced">
         aspirated voiced
        </a>
        ). Many linguists prefer one of the diacritics dedicated to breathy voice over simple aspiration, such as ⟨
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         b̤
        </span>
        ⟩. Some linguists restrict this diacritic to
        <a href="/wiki/Sonorant" title="Sonorant">
         sonorants
        </a>
        , and transcribe obstruents as ⟨
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         bʱ
        </span>
        ⟩.
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Swash">
         <b>
          <a href="#ref_Swash">
           ^b
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        The overstruck tilde is not recommended where it would be typographically unclear. It is also deprecated in Unicode, with precomposed letters preferred. (See
        <a href="/wiki/Pharyngealization" title="Pharyngealization">
         pharyngealization
        </a>
        for available combinations.)
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Subdiacritics (diacritics normally placed below a letter) may be moved above a letter to avoid conflict with a
       <a href="/wiki/Descender" title="Descender">
        descender
       </a>
       , as in voiceless ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ŋ̊
       </span>
       ⟩.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPA15_57-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-IPA15-57">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The raising and lowering diacritics have optional forms ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ˔
       </span>
       ⟩, ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ˕
       </span>
       ⟩ that avoid descenders.
      </p>
      <p>
       The state of the
       <a href="/wiki/Glottis" title="Glottis">
        glottis
       </a>
       can be finely transcribed with diacritics. A series of alveolar plosives ranging from an open to a closed glottis
       <a href="/wiki/Phonation" title="Phonation">
        phonation
       </a>
       are:
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
         Open glottis
        </th>
        <td style="text-align:center; font-size:larger; width:2em;">
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [t]
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiceless" title="Voiceless">
          voiceless
         </a>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td style="text-align:center; font-size:larger;">
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [d̤]
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Breathy_voice" title="Breathy voice">
          breathy voice
         </a>
         , also called
         <i>
          murmured
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td style="text-align:center; font-size:larger;">
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [d̥]
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Slack_voice" title="Slack voice">
          slack voice
         </a>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>

          Sweet spot
         </a>
        </th>
        <td style="text-align:center; font-size:larger;">
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [d]
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Modal_voice" title="Modal voice">
          modal voice
         </a>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td style="text-align:center; font-size:larger;">
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">

         </span>
        </td>

         </span>

        </th>

         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         glottal closure
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Additional diacritics are provided by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extensions_to_the_IPA" title="Extensions to the IPA">
        Extensions to the IPA
       </a>
       for speech pathology.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Suprasegmentals">
        Suprasegmentals
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Suprasegmentals">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       These symbols describe the features of a language above the level of individual consonants and vowels, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Prosody_(linguistics)" title="Prosody (linguistics)">
        prosody
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tone_(linguistics)" title="Tone (linguistics)">
        tone
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Length_(phonetics)" title="Length (phonetics)">
        length
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Stress_(linguistics)" title="Stress (linguistics)">
        stress
       </a>
       , which often operate on syllables, words, or
       <a href="/wiki/Phrase" title="Phrase">
        phrases
       </a>
       : that is, elements such as the intensity, pitch, and gemination of the sounds of a language, as well as the
       <a href="/wiki/Rhythm" title="Rhythm">
        rhythm
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Intonation_(linguistics)" title="Intonation (linguistics)">
        intonation
       </a>
       of speech.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Handbook13_58-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Handbook13-58">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Although most of these symbols indicate distinctions that are phonemic at the word level, symbols also exist for intonation on a level
       <a href="/wiki/Phonological_hierarchy" title="Phonological hierarchy">
        greater than that of the word
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Handbook13_58-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Handbook13-58">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Various ligatures of tone letters are used in the IPA
       <i>
        Handbook
       </i>
       despite not being found on the simplified official IPA chart.
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th colspan="5">

         </a>
        </th>

         <br/>

         </a>

        </td>

         </span>
         .)
        </td>

         </a>
        </td>

         </a>
        </td>

        </th>

       <tr>

        </td>

        </td>

        </td>
        <td style="text-align:center; font-size:larger;">

         </span>

        </td>
       </tr>
