        <td style="text-align:center; font-size:larger;">
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          ŋ̏ ȅ
         </span>
        </td>
        <td style="text-align:center; font-size:larger;">
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          e˩
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         Extra low / bottom
        </td>
        <td style="text-align:center; font-size:larger;">
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          ꜜke
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Downstep_(phonetics)" title="Downstep (phonetics)">
          Downstep
         </a>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Finer distinctions of tone may be indicated by combining the tone diacritics and tone letters shown above, though not all IPA fonts support this. The four additional rising and falling tones supported by diacritics are high/mid rising
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɔ᷄, ɔ˧˥
       </span>
       , low rising
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɔ᷅, ɔ˩˧
       </span>
       , high falling
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɔ᷇, ɔ˥˧
       </span>
       , and low/mid falling
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɔ᷆, ɔ˧˩
       </span>
       . That is, tone diacritics only support contour tones across three levels (high, mid, low), despite supporting five levels for register tones. For other contour tones, tone letters must be used:
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɔ˨˦, ɔ˥˦
       </span>
       , etc. For more complex (peaking and dipping) tones, though it is theoretically possible to combine the three tone diacritics in any permutation, in practice only generic peaking
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɔ᷈
       </span>
       and dipping
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɔ᷉
       </span>
       combinations are used. For finer detail, tone letters are again required (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɔ˧˥˧, ɔ˩˨˩, ɔ˦˩˧, ɔ˨˩˦
       </span>
       , etc.) The correspondence between tone diacritics and tone letters is therefore only approximate.
      </p>
      <p>
       A work-around for diacritics sometimes seen when a language has more than one rising or falling tone, and the author wishes to avoid the poorly legible diacritics
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɔ᷄, ɔ᷅, ɔ᷇, ɔ᷆
       </span>
       but does not wish to completely abandon the IPA, is to restrict generic rising
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɔ̌
       </span>
       and falling
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɔ̂
       </span>
       to the higher-pitched of the rising and falling tones, say
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɔ˥˧
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɔ˧˥
       </span>
       , and to use the old (retired) IPA subscript diacritics
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɔ̗
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɔ̖
       </span>
       for the lower-pitched rising and falling tones, say
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɔ˩˧
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɔ˧˩
       </span>
       . When a language has four or six level tones, the two mid tones are sometimes transcribed as high-mid
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɔ̍
       </span>
       (non-standard) and low-mid
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɔ̄
       </span>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       A stress mark typically appears before the stressed syllable, and thus marks the syllable break as well as stress. Where the syllable onset is a geminate consonant, e.g. in Italian, the consonant is commonly split by the stress mark, which means that the length sign is not used for gemination. (Thus ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        avˈvɔlse
       </span>
       ⟩ not *⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        aˈvvɔlse
       </span>
       ⟩, *⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        aˈvːɔlse
       </span>
       ⟩, or *⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        avˈːɔlse
       </span>
       ⟩.) However, occasionally the stress mark is placed immediately before the stressed vowel, after any syllable onset (⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        avvˈɔlse
       </span>
       ⟩ or ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        avːˈɔlse
       </span>
       ⟩).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In such transcriptions, the stress mark does not function as a mark of the syllable boundary.
      </p>
      <p>
       Tone letters generally appear after each syllable, for a language with syllable tone (⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        a˧vɔ˥˩
       </span>
       ⟩), or after the phonological word, for a language with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Word_tone" title="Word tone">
        word tone
       </a>
       (⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        avɔ˧˥˩
       </span>
       ⟩). However, in older versions of the IPA,
       <i>
        ad hoc
       </i>
       tone marks were placed before the syllable, the same position as used to mark stress, and this convention is still sometimes seen (⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ˧a˥˩vɔ
       </span>
       ⟩, ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ˧˥˩avɔ
       </span>
       ⟩).
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Comparative_degree">
        Comparative degree
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Comparative degree">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       IPA diacritics may be doubled to indicate an extra degree of the feature indicated. This is a productive process, but apart from extra-high and extra-low tones ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ə̋, ə̏
       </span>
       ⟩ being marked by doubled high- and low-tone diacritics, and the major
       <a href="/wiki/Prosodic_unit" title="Prosodic unit">
        prosodic break
       </a>
       ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ‖
       </span>
       ⟩ being marked as a double minor break ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        |
       </span>
       ⟩, it is not specifically regulated by the IPA. (Note that transcription marks are similar: double slashes indicate extra (morpho)-phonemic, double square brackets especially precise, and double parentheses especially unintelligible.)
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       For example, the stress mark may be doubled to indicate an extra degree of stress, such as prosodic stress in English.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
        <a href="#cite_note-61">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       An example in French, with a single stress mark for normal prosodic stress at the end of each
       <a href="/wiki/Prosodic_unit" title="Prosodic unit">
        prosodic unit
       </a>
       (marked as a minor prosodic break), and a double stress mark for contrastive/emphatic stress:
       <br/>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˈˈɑ̃ːˈtre | məˈsjø ‖ ˈˈvwala maˈdam ‖]
       </span>
       <i>
        <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr">
         Entrez monsieur, voilà madame
        </span>
        .
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
        <a href="#cite_note-62">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Similarly, a doubled secondary stress mark ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ˌˌ
       </span>
       ⟩ is commonly used for tertiary stress.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Length_(phonetics)" title="Length (phonetics)">
        Length
       </a>
       is commonly extended by repeating the length mark, as in English
       <i>
        shhh!
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ʃːːː]
       </span>
       , or for "overlong" segments in
       <a href="/wiki/Estonian_phonology" title="Estonian phonology">
        Estonian
       </a>
       :
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         vere
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /vere/
        </span>
        'blood [gen.sg.]',
        <i>
         veere
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /veːre/
        </span>
        'edge [gen.sg.]',
        <i>
         veere
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /veːːre/
        </span>
        'roll [imp. 2nd sg.]'
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         lina
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /linɑ/
        </span>
        'sheet',
        <i>
         linna
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /linːɑ/
        </span>
        'town [gen. sg.]',
        <i>
         linna
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /linːːɑ/
        </span>
        'town [ine. sg.]'
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       (Normally additional degrees of length are handled by the extra-short or half-long diacritics, but in the Estonian examples, the first two cases are analyzed as simply short and long.)
      </p>
      <p>
       Occasionally other diacritics are doubled:
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/R-colored_vowel" title="R-colored vowel">
         Rhoticity
        </a>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/Badaga_language" title="Badaga language">
         Badaga
        </a>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /be/
        </span>
        "mouth",
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /be˞/
        </span>
        "bangle", and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /be˞˞/
        </span>
        "crop".
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
         <a href="#cite_note-63">
          [59]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aspiration_(phonetics)" title="Aspiration (phonetics)">
         Aspiration
        </a>
        , for example contrasting
        <a href="/wiki/Korean_phonology" title="Korean phonology">
         Korean
        </a>
        mild aspiration
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [kʰ]
        </span>
        with strong aspiration
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [kʰʰ]
        </span>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
         <a href="#cite_note-64">
          [60]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Nasal_vowel" title="Nasal vowel">
         Nasalization
        </a>
        , as in
        <a href="/wiki/Palantla_Chinantec" title="Palantla Chinantec">
         Palantla Chinantec
        </a>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ẽ/
        </span>
        vs
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /e͌/
        </span>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
         <a href="#cite_note-65">
          [61]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Weak vs strong
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ejective" title="Ejective">
         ejectives
        </a>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [kʼ]
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [k”]
        </span>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
         <a href="#cite_note-66">
          [62]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Especially lowered, e.g.
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [t̞̞]
        </span>
        (or
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [t̞˕]
        </span>
        , if the former symbol does not display properly) for
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /t/
        </span>
        as a weak fricative in some pronunciations of
        <i>
         register
        </i>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
         <a href="#cite_note-67">
          [63]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Especially retracted (at least on a vowel), e.g.
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ø̠̠]
        </span>
        ,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
         <a href="#cite_note-68">
          [64]
         </a>
        </sup>
        though, depending on the font, on a consonant this could be confused with alveolar or alveolarized notation from the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ExtIPA" title="ExtIPA">
         extIPA
        </a>
        , though such an issue can be easily avoided by placing the second diacritic
        <i>
         to the right of
        </i>
        the letter (
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ø̠˗]
        </span>
        ), rather than below the first diacritic.
       </li>
       <li>
        The transcription of
        <a href="/wiki/Strident_vowel" title="Strident vowel">
         strident
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Harsh_voice" title="Harsh voice">
         harsh voice
        </a>
        as extra-creaky
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /a᷽/
        </span>
        may be motivated by the similarities of these phonations.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Obsolete_and_nonstandard_symbols">
        Obsolete and nonstandard symbols
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Obsolete and nonstandard symbols">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Obsolete_and_nonstandard_symbols_in_the_International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="Obsolete and nonstandard symbols in the International Phonetic Alphabet">
        Obsolete and nonstandard symbols in the International Phonetic Alphabet
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Click_letter" title="Click letter">
        Click letter
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The IPA once had parallel symbols from alternative proposals, but in most cases eventually settled on one for each sound. The rejected symbols are now considered obsolete. An example is the vowel letter ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɷ
       </span>
       ⟩, rejected in favor of ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ʊ
       </span>
       ⟩. Letters for affricates and sounds with inherent secondary articulation have also been mostly rejected, with the idea that such features should be indicated with tie bars or diacritics: ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ƍ
       </span>
       ⟩ for
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [zʷ]
       </span>
       is one. In addition, the rare voiceless implosives, ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ƥ ƭ ƈ ƙ ʠ
       </span>
       ⟩, have been dropped and are now usually written ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɓ̥ ɗ̥ ʄ̊ ɠ̊ ʛ̥
       </span>
       ⟩. A retired set of click letters, ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ʇ, ʗ, ʖ
       </span>
       ⟩, is still sometimes seen, as the official pipe letters ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ǀ, ǃ, ǁ
       </span>
       ⟩ may cause problems with legibility, especially when used with brackets ([ ] or / /), the letter ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        l
       </span>
       ⟩, or the
       <a href="/wiki/Prosody_(linguistics)" title="Prosody (linguistics)">
        prosodic
       </a>
       marks ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        |, ‖
       </span>
       ⟩ (for this reason, some publications which use the current IPA pipe letters disallow IPA brackets).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Individual non-IPA letters may find their way into publications that otherwise use the standard IPA. This is especially common with:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Affricates, such as the Americanist
        <a href="/wiki/Barred_lambda" title="Barred lambda">
         barred lambda
        </a>
        ⟨
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         ƛ
        </span>
        ⟩ for
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [t͜ɬ]
        </span>
        or ⟨
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         č
        </span>
        ⟩ for
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [t͡ʃ]
        </span>
        . Some authors find the tie bars displeasing but the lack of tie bars confusing (i.e. ⟨
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         č
        </span>
        ⟩ for
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /t͡ʃ/
        </span>
        as distinct from
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /tʃ/
        </span>
        ), while others simply prefer to have one letter for each segmental phoneme in a language.
       </li>
       <li>
        Digits for tonal phonemes that have conventional numbers in a local tradition, such as the
        <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese_phonology#Tones" title="Standard Chinese phonology">
         four tones of Standard Chinese
        </a>
        . This may be more convenient for comparison between languages and dialects than a phonetic transcription because tones often vary more than segmental phonemes do.
       </li>
       <li>
        Digits for tone levels, though the lack of standardization can cause confusion (with e.g. "1" for high tone in some languages but for low tone in others).
       </li>
       <li>
        Iconic extensions of standard IPA letters that can be readily understood, such as retroflex
        <a href="/wiki/Voiced_retroflex_implosive" title="Voiced retroflex implosive">
         ⟨ᶑ ⟩
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_retroflex_lateral_fricative" title="Voiceless retroflex lateral fricative">
         ⟨ꞎ⟩
        </a>
        .
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       In addition, there are typewriter substitutions for when IPA support is not available, such as capital ⟨I, E, U, O, A⟩ for
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɪ, ɛ, ʊ, ɔ, ɑ]
       </span>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Extensions">
        Extensions
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Extensions">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Extensions_to_the_International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="Extensions to the International Phonetic Alphabet">
        Extensions to the International Phonetic Alphabet
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The "Extensions to the IPA", often abbreviated as "extIPA" and sometimes called "Extended IPA", are symbols whose original purpose was to accurately transcribe
       <a href="/wiki/Speech_disorder" title="Speech disorder">
        disordered speech
       </a>
       . At the
       <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Association_Kiel_Convention" title="International Phonetic Association Kiel Convention">
        International Phonetic Association Kiel Convention
       </a>
       in 1989, a group of linguists drew up the initial extensions,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
        <a href="#cite_note-70">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which were based on the previous work of the PRDS (Phonetic Representation of Disordered Speech) Group in the early 1980s.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
        <a href="#cite_note-71">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The extensions were first published in 1990, then modified, and published again in 1994 in the
       <i>
        Journal of the International Phonetic Association
       </i>
       , when they were officially adopted by the
       <a href="/wiki/International_Clinical_Phonetics_and_Linguistics_Association" title="International Clinical Phonetics and Linguistics Association">
        ICPLA
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
        <a href="#cite_note-72">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       While the original purpose was to transcribe disordered speech, linguists have used the extensions to designate a number of unique sounds within standard communication, such as hushing, gnashing teeth, and smacking lips. The extensions have also been used to record certain peculiarities in an individual's voice, such as nasalized voicing.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-world_2-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-world-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Extensions to the IPA do not include symbols used for voice quality (
       <a href="/wiki/Voice_Quality_Symbols" title="Voice Quality Symbols">
        Voice Quality Symbols
       </a>
       ), such as whispering.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Segments_without_letters">
        Segments without letters
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Segments without letters">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The remaining blank cells on the IPA chart can be filled without too much difficulty if the need arises. Some
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Ad_hoc" title="Ad hoc">
         ad hoc
        </a>
       </i>
       letters have appeared in the literature for the retroflex lateral flap, the voiceless lateral fricatives, the epiglottal trill, and the labiodental plosives. (See the grey letters in the PDF chart.) Diacritics can supply much of the remainder.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
        <a href="#cite_note-73">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       If a sound cannot be transcribed, an asterisk ⟨*⟩ may be used, either as a letter or as a diacritic (as in ⟨k*⟩ sometimes seen for the
       <a href="/wiki/Korean_phonology" title="Korean phonology">
        Korean
       </a>
       'fortis' velar).
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Consonants_2">
        Consonants
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Consonants">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Representations of consonant sounds outside of the core set are created by adding diacritics to letters with similar sound values. The Spanish bilabial and dental approximants are commonly written as lowered fricatives,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [β̞]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ð̞]
       </span>
       respectively. Similarly, voiced lateral fricatives would be written as raised lateral approximants,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɭ˔ ʎ̝ ʟ̝]
       </span>
       . A few languages such as
       <a href="/wiki/Banda_languages" title="Banda languages">
        Banda
       </a>
       have a bilabial flap as the preferred allophone of what is elsewhere a labiodental flap. It has been suggested that this be written with the labiodental flap letter and the advanced diacritic,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ⱱ̟]
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
        <a href="#cite_note-74">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Similarly, a labiodental trill would be written
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ʙ̪]
       </span>
       (bilabial trill and the dental sign), and labiodental stops
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [p̪ b̪]
       </span>
       rather than with the
       <i>
        ad hoc
       </i>
       letters sometimes found in the literature. Other taps can be written as extra-short plosives or laterals, e.g.
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɟ̆ ɢ̆/ʀ̆ ʟ̆]
       </span>
       , though in some cases the diacritic would need to be written below the letter. A retroflex trill can be written as a retracted
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [r̠]
       </span>
       , just as retroflex fricatives sometimes are. The remaining consonants, the uvular laterals (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ʟ̠
       </span>
       <i>
        etc.
       </i>
       ) and the palatal trill, while not strictly impossible, are very difficult to pronounce and are unlikely to occur even as allophones in the world's languages.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels_2">
        Vowels
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Vowels">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The vowels are similarly manageable by using diacritics for raising, lowering, fronting, backing, centering, and mid-centering.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
        <a href="#cite_note-75">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       For example, the unrounded equivalent of
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ʊ]
       </span>
       can be transcribed as mid-centered
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɯ̽]
       </span>
       , and the rounded equivalent of
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [æ]
       </span>
       as raised
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɶ̝]
       </span>
       or lowered
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [œ̞]
       </span>
       . True mid vowels are lowered
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [e̞ ø̞ ɘ̞ ɵ̞ ɤ̞ o̞]
       </span>
       or raised
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɛ̝ œ̝ ɜ̝ ɞ̝ ʌ̝ ɔ̝]
       </span>
       , while centered
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɪ̈ ʊ̈]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ä]
       </span>
       (or, less commonly,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɑ̈]
       </span>
       ) are near-close and open central vowels, respectively. The only known vowels that cannot be represented in this scheme are vowels with unexpected
       <a href="/wiki/Roundedness" title="Roundedness">
        roundedness
       </a>
       , which would require a dedicated diacritic, such as ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ʏʷ
       </span>
       ⟩ and ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        uᵝ
       </span>
       ⟩ (or ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɪʷ
       </span>
       ⟩ and ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɯᵝ
       </span>
       ⟩).
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Symbol_names">
        Symbol names
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Symbol names">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Naming_conventions_of_the_International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="Naming conventions of the International Phonetic Alphabet">
        Naming conventions of the International Phonetic Alphabet
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       An IPA symbol is often distinguished from the sound it is intended to represent, since there is not necessarily a one-to-one correspondence between letter and sound in broad transcription, making articulatory descriptions such as 'mid front rounded vowel' or 'voiced velar stop' unreliable. While the
       <i>
        Handbook of the International Phonetic Association
       </i>
       states that no official names exist for its symbols, it admits the presence of one or two common names for each.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
        <a href="#cite_note-76">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The symbols also have
       <a href="/wiki/Nonce_word" title="Nonce word">
        nonce names
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">
        Unicode
       </a>
       standard. In some cases, the Unicode names and the IPA names do not agree. For example, IPA calls
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɛ
       </span>
       "epsilon", but Unicode calls it "small letter open E".
      </p>
      <p>
       The traditional names of the Latin and Greek letters are usually used for unmodified letters.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
        <a href="#cite_note-77">
         [note 5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Letters which are not directly derived from these alphabets, such as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [
        <a href="/wiki/Voiced_pharyngeal_fricative" title="Voiced pharyngeal fricative">
         ʕ
        </a>
        ]
       </span>
       , may have a variety of names, sometimes based on the appearance of the symbol or on the sound that it represents. In Unicode, some of the letters of Greek origin have Latin forms for use in IPA; the others use the letters from the Greek section.
      </p>
      <p>
       For diacritics, there are two methods of naming. For traditional diacritics, the IPA notes the name in a well known language; for example,
       <strong>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         é
        </span>
       </strong>
       is
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Acute_accent" title="Acute accent">
         acute
        </a>
       </i>
       , based on the name of the diacritic in English and French. Non-traditional diacritics are often named after objects they resemble, so
       <strong>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         d̪
        </span>
       </strong>
       is called
       <i>
        bridge
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Pullum" title="Geoffrey Pullum">
        Geoffrey Pullum
       </a>
       and
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=William_Ladusaw&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="William Ladusaw (page does not exist)">
        William Ladusaw
       </a>
       list a variety of names in use for IPA symbols, both current and retired, in addition to names of many other non-IPA phonetic symbols.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pullum_11-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Pullum-11">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Their collection is extensive enough that the
       <a href="/wiki/Unicode_Consortium" title="Unicode Consortium">
        Unicode Consortium
       </a>
       used it in the development of
       <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">
        Unicode
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Typefaces">
        Typefaces
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Typefaces">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       IPA typeface support is increasing, and is now included in several typefaces such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Times_New_Roman" title="Times New Roman">
        Times New Roman
       </a>
       versions that come with various recent computer
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Operating_systems" title="Operating systems">
        operating systems
       </a>
       . Diacritics are not always properly rendered, however. IPA typefaces that are freely available online include
       <a href="/wiki/Gentium" title="Gentium">
        Gentium
       </a>
       , several from the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Summer_Institute_of_Linguistics" title="Summer Institute of Linguistics">
        SIL
       </a>
       (such as
       <a href="/wiki/Charis_SIL" title="Charis SIL">
        Charis SIL
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Doulos_SIL" title="Doulos SIL">
        Doulos SIL
       </a>
       ),
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Dehuti&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Dehuti (page does not exist)">
        Dehuti
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/DejaVu_fonts" title="DejaVu fonts">
        DejaVu Sans
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Bitstream_Cyberbit" title="Bitstream Cyberbit">
        TITUS Cyberbit
       </a>
       , which are all
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_software_Unicode_typefaces" title="Free software Unicode typefaces">
        freely available
       </a>
       ; as well as commercial typefaces such as Brill, available from
       <a href="/wiki/Brill_Publishers" title="Brill Publishers">
        Brill Publishers
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Lucida_Sans_Unicode" title="Lucida Sans Unicode">
        Lucida Sans Unicode
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Arial_Unicode_MS" title="Arial Unicode MS">
        Arial Unicode MS
       </a>
       , shipping with various
       <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">
        Microsoft
       </a>
       products. These all include several ranges of characters in addition to the IPA. Modern
       <a href="/wiki/Web_browser" title="Web browser">
        Web browsers
       </a>
       generally do not need any configuration to display these symbols, provided that a typeface capable of doing so is available to the operating system.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="ASCII_and_keyboard_transliterations">
        ASCII and keyboard transliterations
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: ASCII and keyboard transliterations">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Several systems have been developed that map the IPA symbols to
       <a href="/wiki/ASCII" title="ASCII">
        ASCII
       </a>
       characters. Notable systems include
       <a href="/wiki/Kirshenbaum" title="Kirshenbaum">
        Kirshenbaum
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/ARPABET" title="ARPABET">
        ARPABET
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SAMPA" title="SAMPA">
        SAMPA
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/X-SAMPA" title="X-SAMPA">
        X-SAMPA
       </a>
       . The usage of mapping systems in on-line text has to some extent been adopted in the context input methods, allowing convenient keying of IPA characters that would be otherwise unavailable on standard keyboard layouts.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Computer_input_using_on-screen_keyboard">
        Computer input using on-screen keyboard
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Computer input using on-screen keyboard">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Online IPA keyboard utilities
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
        <a href="#cite_note-78">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       are available and they cover the (complete) range of IPA symbols and diacritics.
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">
        Notes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Phonetic_Alphabet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Notes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
       <ol class="references">
        <li id="cite_note-4">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-4">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          The inverted bridge under the ⟨t⟩ specifies it as
          <a href="/wiki/Apical_consonant" title="Apical consonant">
           apical
          </a>
          (pronounced with the tip of the tongue), and the superscript
          <i>
           h
          </i>
          shows that it is
          <a href="/wiki/Aspirated_consonant" title="Aspirated consonant">
           aspirated
          </a>
          (breathy). Both these qualities cause the English
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           [t]
          </span>
          to sound different from the French or Spanish
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           [t]
          </span>
          , which is a
          <a href="/wiki/Laminal_consonant" title="Laminal consonant">
           laminal
          </a>
          (pronounced with the blade of the tongue) and unaspirated
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           [t̻]
          </span>
          . ⟨
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           t̺ʰ
          </span>
          ⟩ and ⟨
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           t̻
          </span>
          ⟩ are thus two different IPA symbols for two different, though similar, sounds.
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-15">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-15">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          For instance,
          <a href="/wiki/Flap_consonant" title="Flap consonant">
           flaps and taps
          </a>
          are two different kinds of
          <a href="/wiki/Manner_of_articulation" title="Manner of articulation">
           articulation
          </a>
          , but since no language has (yet) been found to make a distinction between, say, an
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_tap" title="Alveolar tap">
           alveolar flap
          </a>
          and an alveolar tap, the IPA does not provide such sounds with dedicated letters. Instead, it provides a single letter (in this case,
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           [ɾ]
          </span>
          ) for both. Strictly speaking, this makes the IPA a partially
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phonemic" title="Phonemic">
           phon
           <i>
            em
           </i>
           ic
          </a>
          alphabet, not a purely
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phonetic" title="Phonetic">
           phon
           <i>
            et
           </i>
           ic
          </a>
          one.
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-16">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-16">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          There are five basic tone diacritics and five basic tone letters, both sets of which are compounded for contour tones.
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-17">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-17">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          "The non-roman letters of the International Phonetic Alphabet have been designed as far as possible to harmonize well with the roman letters. The Association does not recognize makeshift letters; It recognizes only letters which have been carefully cut so as to be in harmony with the other letters." (IPA 1949)
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-77">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-77">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          For example,
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           [p]
          </span>
          is called "Lower-case P" and
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           [χ]
          </span>
          is "Chi." (International Phonetic Association,
          <i>
           Handbook
          </i>
          , p. 171)
         </span>
        </li>
       </ol>
      </div>

            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Proper angle brackets used in transcription are the mathematical symbols ⟨...⟩ (U+27E8 and U+27E9). These may not be supported by older fonts. Chevrons ‹...› (U+2039, U+203A) are sometimes substituted, as are the less-than and greater-than signs &lt;...&gt; (U+003C, U+003E) found on ASCII keyboards.

            ^

         <span class="reference-text">

            It is traditional to place the tie bar above the letters. It may be placed below to avoid overlap with ascenders or diacritic marks, or simply because it is more legible that way, as in Niesler, Louw, &amp; Roux (2005)
           </q>

         <span class="reference-text">
          The global rise and fall arrows come before the affected syllable or prosodic unit, like stress and upstep/downstep. This contrasts with the Chao tone letters, which come after.
